Life of Imam Ibn Asakir
beginner
4 min
Arrival and Burial in Isfarayin
al-Marandi narrates al-Baghdadi's move, death, and burial next to al-Isfarayini.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the warm reception Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi received in Isfarayin and the significance of his burial next to Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini.

Core Idea
Scholars are like stars gathered in the sky of knowledge.

This short passage describes Abu Mansur's move to Isfarayin, his death soon after, and his burial next to the great scholar Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini. The poetic comparison of their graves to stars emphasizes their joint legacy.

Guided Path
1
Read the arrival account
Read how the people of Isfarayin rejoiced at al-Baghdadi's arrival, showing his esteemed reputation.
Note the phrase 'ابتهج الناس بمقدمه إلى الحد الذي لا يوصف'.
2
Read the burial description
Notice that he was buried next to Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, with the graves touching. The imagery of stars and a rising point is used.
The phrase 'كأنهما نجمان جمعهما مطلع' is a beautiful metaphor.
3
Consider the reason for moving
The passage mentions that Isfarayin was more stable during the Turkoman troubles, which is why he moved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حدثنى الشيخ أبو بكر يحيى بن إبراهيم بن أحمد بن محمد السلماسى عن أبيه القاضي أبي طاهر قال قال أبو على الحسن بن نصر بن كاكا المرندى الفقيه في ذكر أبي عثمان الصابوني أنه ذكر أبا منصور المتكلم ، قال أبو على وكنت قد أهملت ذكر اسمه ونسبه اعتماداً على شهرته فقال لى أبو عثمان قيد ذكره بإثبات اسمه وأزل الشبهة عن فأثبت فوق الكنية عبد القاهر بن طاهر لئلا يظن أنك أردت أبا منصور الآخر ، فكأنه أشار إلى خلاف في الاعتقاد كان بينهما ومهما نفيت الاحتمال والشركة ورفعت الظن والشبهة بان أنى أردت ببياني أبا منصور البغدادي ثم قال أبو عثمان كان من أئمة الأصول وصدور الإسلام بإجماع أهل الفضل والتحصيل ، بديع الترتيب غريب التأليف في التهذيب يراه الجلة صدرا مقدما ويدعوه الأئمة إماما مفخما ومن خراب نيسابور أن اضطر مثله إلى مفارقتها إلى حيث خلق منه وتوفی باسفراین وبها قبره رحمه الله
وقال أبو على المرندى وحدثني أبو عبد قال لما حصل أبو منصور باسفراين ابتهج الناس بمقدمه إلى الحد الذي لا يوصف، فلم يبق إلا يسيرا حتى مات واتفق أهل العلم على دفنه يجنب أبى إسحق إبراهيم بن محمد المتكلم الاسفراينى فقبراهما متجاوران تجاور تلاصق كأنهما نجمان جمعهما مطلع وكوكبان ضمهما برج مرتفع قال : وإنما انتقل إلى أسفراين لأن حالها كان بعد متماسكاً بعض التماسك
كتب إلى الشيخ أبو الحسن عبد الغافر بن إسماعيل النيسابوري قال فی ذیل تاریخ نيسابور : عبد القاهر بن طاهر بن محمد البغدادى أبو الأستاذ الإمام الكامل ذو الفنون الفقيه الأصولي الأديب الشاعر النحوى الماهر في علم الحساب ، العارف بالعروض ورد نيسابور مع أبيه أبي طاهر وكان ذا مال وثروة ومروءة وتفقه على أهل العلم والحديث وأنفق ماله على أهل العلم حتى افتقر صنف في العلوم وأربى على أقرانه في الفنون ودرس فى سبعة عشر نوعاً من العلوم وكان قد درس على الاستاذ أبي إسحاق الاسفراينى وأقعده بعده في مسجد عقيل للاملاء مكانه وأملى سنين واختلف إليه الأئمة فقروا عليه مثل الإمام ناصر المروزي وأبى القسم القشيري وغيرهما وحدث عن الإسماعيلي وأبي أحمد بن عبدی خرج من نيسابور في أيام التركمانية وفتنتهم إلى أسفراين فمات بها سنة تسع وعشرين وأربعماية .
Shaykh Abu Bakr Yahya ibn Ibrahim ibn Ahmad ibn Muhammad al-Salamasi narrated to me from his father, the judge Abu Tahir, who said: Abu Ali al-Hasan ibn Nasr ibn Kaka al-Marandi the faqih said, in mentioning Abu Uthman al-Sabuni, that he mentioned Abu Mansur the mutakallim (theologian). Abu Ali said: I had neglected to mention his name and lineage, relying on his fame. So Abu Uthman said to me: 'Restrict his mention by setting down his name and remove ambiguity.' So I set down above the kunyah (patronymic) 'Abd al-Qahir ibn Tahir, lest it be thought that you meant the other Abu Mansur. It was as if he pointed to a disagreement in belief that was between them. And whenever you negate the possibility and association and raise the suspicion and ambiguity, it becomes clear that I intended by my statement Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi. Then Abu Uthman said: 'He was among the imams of usul (principles of religion) and the foremost of Islam by consensus of the people of virtue and attainment. Wonderful in arrangement, unique in composition in refinement. The great ones see him as a foremost leader, and the imams call him a magnified imam. And from the ruin of Nishapur is that one like him was compelled to leave it to where he was created, and he died in Isfarayin, and there is his grave, may God have mercy on him.' And Abu Ali al-Marandi said: Abu Abdullah narrated to me, saying: When Abu Mansur arrived in Isfarayin, the people rejoiced at his arrival to an extent that cannot be described. He remained only a short time until he died. The people of knowledge agreed to bury him next to Abu Ishaq Ibrahim ibn Muhammad the mutakallim al-Isfarayini. Their two graves are adjacent, attached, as if they are two stars joined by a rising point, and two planets gathered by a lofty tower. He said: He only moved to Isfarayin because its condition was still somewhat stable. The Shaykh Abu al-Hasan Abd al-Ghafir ibn Ismail al-Naysaburi wrote to me, saying in the supplement to the History of Nishapur: 'Abd al-Qahir ibn Tahir ibn Muhammad al-Baghdadi, Abu al-Ustadh (father of the master), the complete imam, possessor of many arts, the faqih, the usuli, the adib, the poet, the grammarian, the skilled in arithmetic, the knower of prosody. He came to Nishapur with his father Abu Tahir, and he had wealth, riches, and chivalry. He studied fiqh with the people of knowledge and hadith. He spent his wealth on the people of knowledge until he became poor. He authored works in the sciences and excelled his peers in the arts. He taught in seventeen types of sciences. He had studied under Ustadh Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, and after him he appointed him in the Mosque of 'Uqayl for dictation in his place, and he dictated for years. The imams would frequent him and read to him, such as Imam Nasir al-Marwazi and Abu al-Qasim al-Qushayri and others. He narrated from al-Isma'ili and Abu Ahmad ibn 'Abd [something]. He left Nishapur during the days of the Turkomans and their strife to Isfarayin, and he died there in the year 429 AH.'
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part biographical entry for Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi (Abd al-Qahir ibn Tahir). It begins with a narrative from al-Marandi and al-Sabuni, emphasizing the need to precisely identify Abu Mansur by his full name and kunyah to avoid confusion with another scholar of similar name. Al-Sabuni describes him as an imam of usul and a foremost scholar of Islam, praising his works and lamenting the necessity of his departure from Nishapur. The second part describes his warm reception in Isfarayin and his burial next to Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, symbolically comparing their graves to stars. The third part is a formal biographical notice from Abd al-Ghafir al-Naysaburi, detailing his vast knowledge across seventeen sciences, his teachers, his students (including Nasir al-Marwazi and al-Qushayri), his generosity, and his eventual death in Isfarayin in 429 AH due to Turkoman strife.

Key Terms
أبو على المرندى
أبو إسحاق الإسفرايني
أسفراين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Where was Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi buried?
A) In Nishapur
B) Next to Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini
C) In Baghdad
D) In a separate grave alone

Key Takeaway
Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi was buried next to Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini in Isfarayin, symbolizing their close scholarly companionship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you felt overwhelming joy at the presence of a true scholar of Islam? What does that reveal about your heart's connection to sacred knowledge? Write down one step you will take this week to seek out such company.
?
The graves of these two scholars are described as stars that never fade. Imagine your own grave: what would the people who gather there say about your life? Are you building a legacy of light that will remain after you? What will you change today to ensure it?

Life of Imam Ibn Asakir
beginner
6 min
Formal Biography of Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi
al-Naysaburi provides a comprehensive biographical entry detailing al-Baghdadi's learning, teachers, students, and death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will appreciate the breadth of Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi's scholarship, his teachers and students, and the circumstances of his death.

Core Idea
A master of seventeen sciences, generous to the poor.

This passage is a formal biographical entry from Abd al-Ghafir al-Naysaburi's history of Nishapur. It details al-Baghdadi's expertise in multiple disciplines, his study under Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, his illustrious students like al-Qushayri, and his death in Isfarayin due to Turkoman strife.

Guided Path
1
Read the biography
Read the full description of al-Baghdadi's learning, his mastery of seventeen sciences, and his teaching.
Notice the list of sciences: فقيه, أصولي, أديب, شاعر, نحوي, ماهر في الحساب, عارف بالعروض.
2
Identify his teachers and students
He studied under Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini and taught Nasir al-Marwazi and Abu al-Qasim al-Qushayri.
These are major figures in Ash'ari and Sufi tradition.
3
Note his generosity
He spent his wealth on scholars until he became poor. This shows his selflessness.
4
Understand his departure
He left Nishapur due to Turkoman violence and died in Isfarayin in 429 AH.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حدثنى الشيخ أبو بكر يحيى بن إبراهيم بن أحمد بن محمد السلماسى عن أبيه القاضي أبي طاهر قال قال أبو على الحسن بن نصر بن كاكا المرندى الفقيه في ذكر أبي عثمان الصابوني أنه ذكر أبا منصور المتكلم ، قال أبو على وكنت قد أهملت ذكر اسمه ونسبه اعتماداً على شهرته فقال لى أبو عثمان قيد ذكره بإثبات اسمه وأزل الشبهة عن فأثبت فوق الكنية عبد القاهر بن طاهر لئلا يظن أنك أردت أبا منصور الآخر ، فكأنه أشار إلى خلاف في الاعتقاد كان بينهما ومهما نفيت الاحتمال والشركة ورفعت الظن والشبهة بان أنى أردت ببياني أبا منصور البغدادي ثم قال أبو عثمان كان من أئمة الأصول وصدور الإسلام بإجماع أهل الفضل والتحصيل ، بديع الترتيب غريب التأليف في التهذيب يراه الجلة صدرا مقدما ويدعوه الأئمة إماما مفخما ومن خراب نيسابور أن اضطر مثله إلى مفارقتها إلى حيث خلق منه وتوفی باسفراین وبها قبره رحمه الله
وقال أبو على المرندى وحدثني أبو عبد قال لما حصل أبو منصور باسفراين ابتهج الناس بمقدمه إلى الحد الذي لا يوصف، فلم يبق إلا يسيرا حتى مات واتفق أهل العلم على دفنه يجنب أبى إسحق إبراهيم بن محمد المتكلم الاسفراينى فقبراهما متجاوران تجاور تلاصق كأنهما نجمان جمعهما مطلع وكوكبان ضمهما برج مرتفع قال : وإنما انتقل إلى أسفراين لأن حالها كان بعد متماسكاً بعض التماسك
كتب إلى الشيخ أبو الحسن عبد الغافر بن إسماعيل النيسابوري قال فی ذیل تاریخ نيسابور : عبد القاهر بن طاهر بن محمد البغدادى أبو الأستاذ الإمام الكامل ذو الفنون الفقيه الأصولي الأديب الشاعر النحوى الماهر في علم الحساب ، العارف بالعروض ورد نيسابور مع أبيه أبي طاهر وكان ذا مال وثروة ومروءة وتفقه على أهل العلم والحديث وأنفق ماله على أهل العلم حتى افتقر صنف في العلوم وأربى على أقرانه في الفنون ودرس فى سبعة عشر نوعاً من العلوم وكان قد درس على الاستاذ أبي إسحاق الاسفراينى وأقعده بعده في مسجد عقيل للاملاء مكانه وأملى سنين واختلف إليه الأئمة فقروا عليه مثل الإمام ناصر المروزي وأبى القسم القشيري وغيرهما وحدث عن الإسماعيلي وأبي أحمد بن عبدی خرج من نيسابور في أيام التركمانية وفتنتهم إلى أسفراين فمات بها سنة تسع وعشرين وأربعماية .

The imams would frequent him and read to him, such as Imam Nasir al-Marwazi and Abu al-Qasim al-Qushayri and others. He narrated from al-Isma'ili and Abu Ahmad ibn 'Abd [something]. He left Nishapur during the days of the Turkomans and their strife to Isfarayin, and he died there in the year 429 AH.'

Commentary

It begins with a narrative from al-Marandi and al-Sabuni, emphasizing the need to precisely identify Abu Mansur by his full name and kunyah to avoid confusion with another scholar of similar name. Al-Sabuni describes him as an imam of usul and a foremost scholar of Islam, praising his works and lamenting the necessity of his departure from Nishapur. The second part describes his warm reception in Isfarayin and his burial next to Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, symbolically comparing their graves to stars. In how many sciences did Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi teach?
A) Seven

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Baghdadi gave away his entire wealth to support scholars until he became poor. Is there a possession or comfort you cling to that prevents you from fully supporting the cause of knowledge? What would it take for you to let it go today?
?
He mastered seventeen sciences while also teaching and writing. What is one distraction in your daily routine that you could replace with a consistent act of learning or teaching for Allah? Will you commit to that change starting this week?
?
The passage notes that al-Baghdadi left Nishapur due to violence but continued his work until death. When hardship strikes, do you abandon your religious duties or does your devotion deepen? Be honest — and decide now what you will do the next time difficulty arises.

Life of Imam Ibn Asakir

Abu Dharr al-Harawi: Biography and Conversion to Ash'arism
Ibn Asakir narrates the biography of Abu Dharr al-Harawi, his travels, death, and conversion to the Ash'ari school via al-Baqillani.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the biographical details of Abu Dharr al-Harawi and the story of his conversion to the Ash'ari and Maliki schools through his encounter with al-Baqillani.

Core Idea
Personal scholarly encounters can shape one's entire theological orientation.

Ibn Asakir presents the biography of a prominent hadith memorizer, Abu Dharr al-Harawi, who traveled widely and settled in Mecca. More importantly, he includes a story that explains why a scholar from Herat became a follower of the Maliki school and the Ash'ari theological tradition. The narrative centers on a moment of profound respect shown by al-Daraqutni towards Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, which inspired Abu Dharr's adherence.

Guided Path
1
Read the biographical details
Begin by reading the biographical section about Abu Dharr's travels, birth, death, and scholarly credentials. Note his trustworthiness and his long residence in Mecca.
The text provides two chains of transmission for this information.
2
Identify the key encounter
Pay attention to the story of his conversion. What did al-Daraqutni do that amazed Abu Dharr? Who was the person honored?
The person honored is the qadi Abu Bakr ibn al-Tayyib, known as al-Baqillani.
3
Understand the result
Reflect on how this single encounter led Abu Dharr to adopt the Ash'ari and Maliki schools.
The story shows the power of scholarly veneration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يا من عدا ثم اعتدى ثم اقترف ثم انتهى ثم ارعوى ثم اعترف أبشر بقول الله في آياته إن ينتهوا يغفر لهم ما قد سلف ومنهم أبو ذر الهروى الحافظ رحمه الله ) أخبرنا الشيخان أبو الحسن على بن أحمد الفقيه وأبو منصور ابن عبد الملك المقرى قالا قال لنا أبو بكر أحمد بن على بن ثابت الحافظ أحمد بن محمد أبو ذر الهروى سافر الكثير وحدث ببغداد عن أبي الفضل بن خميرويه الهروى وأبي منصور النضروى وبسر بن محمد المزني وطبقتهم وكنت لما حدث غائبا خرج أبو ذر إلى مكة فسكنها مدة ثم تزوج في العرب وأقام بالسروات وكان يحج في كل عام ويقيم بمكة أيام الموسم ويحدث ثم يرجع إلى أهله وكتب إلينا من مكة بالإجازة بجميع حديثه وكان ثقة فاضلاً ضابطاً دينا وكان يذكر أن مولده في سنة خمس أو ست وخمسين وثلاثماية يشك في ذلك ومات بمكة لخمس خلون من ذي القعدة سنة أربع وثلاثين وأربعمائة أخبرنا الشيخ أبو محمد هبة الله بن أحمد الأكفاني قال حدثني أبو على الحسين بن أحمد ابن أبي حريصة قال بلغني أن أبا ذر عبد بن أحمد الهروى الحافظ رحمه الله توفي في شهور سنة أربع وثلاثين وأربعمائة وكان مقيماً بمكة وبها مات وكان على مذهب مالك وعلى مذهب أبي الحسن الأشعري سمعت الشيخ الحافظ أبا الحسن على بن سليمان بن أحمد الأندلسي يقول سمعت أبا على الحسن بن على الأنصاري البطليوسي يقول سمعت أبا على الحسن بن إبراهيم بن تقى الجذامي المالقي يقول سمعت بعض الشيوخ يقول قيل لأبي الهروى أنت من هراة فمن أين تمذهبت لمالك والأشعرى فقال سبب ذلك أني قدمت بغداد لطلب الحديث فلزمت الدارقطني فلما كان في بعض الأيام كنت معه فاجتاز به القاضي أبو بكر بن الطيب فأظهر الدارقطني من إكرامه ما تعجبت منه فلما فارقه قلت له أيها الشيخ الإمام من هذا الذي أظهرت من إكرامه ما رأيت فقال أو ما تعرفه قلت لا فقال هذا سيف السنة أبو بكر الأشعري فلزمت القاضي منذ ذلك واقتديت به في مذهبه جميعا أو كما قال
O you who transgressed, then exceeded bounds, then committed sins, then desisted, then relented, then confessed – rejoice in the saying of Allah in His verses: 'If they desist, what is past will be forgiven them' (Quran 8:38). And among them is Abu Dharr al-Harawi, the memorizer, may Allah have mercy on him. The two shaykhs Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Ahmad al-Faqih and Abu Mansur ibn Abd al-Malik al-Muqri informed us, saying: Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Ali ibn Thabit al-Hafiz told us: Ahmad ibn Muhammad Abu Dharr al-Harawi traveled extensively and narrated in Baghdad from Abu al-Fadl ibn Khumayruwayh al-Harawi, Abu Mansur al-Nadrawi, Busr ibn Muhammad al-Muzani, and their class. When he narrated, I was absent. Abu Dharr went out to Mecca and resided there for a time, then married among the Arabs and settled in al-Sarawat. He would perform the Hajj every year, stay in Mecca during the days of the season, narrate [hadith], then return to his family. He wrote to us from Mecca with a license to transmit all his hadith. He was trustworthy, virtuous, precise, and religious. He mentioned that his birth was in the year 355 or 356 AH – he was uncertain about that – and he died in Mecca on the fifth of Dhu al-Qa'da in the year 434 AH. The shaykh Abu Muhammad Hibatallah ibn Ahmad al-Akfani informed us, saying: Abu Ali al-Husayn ibn Ahmad ibn Abi Harisa narrated to me, saying: I was informed that Abu Dharr Abd ibn Ahmad al-Harawi al-Hafiz, may Allah have mercy on him, died in the months of the year 434 AH, and he was residing in Mecca – and there he died. He followed the school of Malik and the school of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. I heard the shaykh al-hafiz Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Sulayman ibn Ahmad al-Andalusi say: I heard Abu Ali al-Hasan ibn Ali al-Ansari al-Batliyusi say: I heard Abu Ali al-Hasan ibn Ibrahim ibn Taqi al-Judhami al-Malaqi say: I heard some shaykhs say: It was said to Abu Dharr al-Harawi: 'You are from Herat, so from where did you adopt the school of Malik and al-Ash'ari?' He said: 'The reason for that is that I came to Baghdad seeking hadith and I attached myself to al-Daraqutni. One day when I was with him, the qadi Abu Bakr ibn al-Tayyib passed by him, and al-Daraqutni showed him such honor that I was amazed. When he left, I said to him: "O shaykh and imam, who is this to whom you showed such honor as I saw?" He said: "Do you not know him?" I said: "No." He said: "This is the sword of the Sunnah, Abu Bakr al-Ash'ari." So I attached myself to the qadi from that time and followed him in his entire school – or as he said.'
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry from Ibn Asakir's work. It begins with a general exhortation quoting the Quranic verse 8:38, addressing someone who has repented. Then it introduces one of the scholars: Abu Dharr al-Harawi. The passage provides his full name, his scholarly credentials, his extensive travels, his teaching in Baghdad, and his eventual settlement in Mecca. It gives his birth and death dates, noting uncertainty about the year of birth. A second chain of transmission confirms his death and his adherence to both the Maliki school of fiqh and the Ash'ari school of theology. The passage then includes a lengthy chain of narrators leading to a story that explains how Abu Dharr came to adopt these two schools. According to the story, when asked about his adoption of Maliki and Ash'ari positions, Abu Dharr related that while studying with al-Daraqutni in Baghdad, he witnessed al-Daraqutni showing extraordinary honor to a passing qadi, Abu Bakr ibn al-Tayyib (al-Baqillani). Upon inquiry, al-Daraqutni called him 'the sword of the Sunnah'. Impressed, Abu Dharr attached himself to al-Baqillani and adopted his school, which is the Ash'ari school. This highlights the role of personal encounter and scholarly veneration in shaping one's theological commitments.

Key Terms
أبو ذر الهروى
الدارقطني
القاضي أبو بكر بن الطيب
سيف السنة
مذهب مالك
مذهب أبي الحسن الأشعري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Abu Dharr was originally a Hanbali or Shafi'i before converting to Ash'arism and Maliki.
Correction: The passage indicates that Abu Dharr adopted the Maliki and Ash'ari schools after his encounter with al-Baqillani, not that he converted from another school.

Checkpoint
According to the story, who did Abu Dharr see being honored by al-Daraqutni, which led him to follow the Ash'ari school?
A) al-Ash'ari
B) al-Baqillani (Abu Bakr ibn al-Tayyib)
C) al-Juwayni
D) al-Ghazali

Key Takeaway
A single moment of witnessing reverence for a scholar can lead to a lifelong commitment to a school of thought.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone you respect showing honor and deference to a particular scholar or teacher, does that affect your own view of that person? Would you be willing to adopt a whole school of thought based solely on that kind of encounter? What does that say about the power of perceived authority in your own learning?
?
Abu Dharr traveled far for knowledge and yet his theological allegiance was decided in one moment of watching al-Daraqutni honor al-Baqillani. Think carefully: Who in your life are you watching and learning from? Have you ever considered that your own theological or legal tendencies might be shaped more by personal admiration than by careful study of the evidence?
?
The source calls al-Baqillani 'the sword of the Sunnah'. Do you know who defends the Sunnah today? What would it take for you to seek out and attach yourself to such a person? Are you prepared to let your heart be moved by reverence for truth, even if it means changing a long-held position?

Life of Imam Ibn Asakir

The Power of a Scholar's Supplication: Ibn al-Jarmi and the Tyrant
Ibn Asakir relates the life of Abu Bakr ibn al-Jarmi, his Ash'ari affiliation, and his brave act of pouring out wine and his supplication against the tyrant Jish.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the supplication of a righteous scholar can bring divine justice against a tyrant, as exemplified by the story of Abu Bakr ibn al-Jarmi and Governor Jish.

Core Idea
A scholar's single supplication can shake a tyrant's throne.

This lesson presents a vivid biographical account from Ibn Asakir's Tabyin Kidb al-Muftari. It narrates the courageous act of Abu Bakr ibn al-Jarmi, an Ash'ari scholar, who fearlessly poured out wine intended for the tyrannical governor Jish. The story culminates in the governor's horrific illness and his own admission that a single arrow (symbolizing the scholar's supplication) struck him, showing the power of righteous prayer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the story's flow and details come through before interpreting.
Focus on the sequence of events: background, wine-pouring, trial, illness, and dream.
2
Identify the character traits
Notice how Ibn al-Jarmi is described: his asceticism, his Ash'ari school, his role as an enjoiner of good. Contrast with Jish's tyranny and oppression.
These traits set up the moral contrast.
3
Examine the trial scene
Observe how Jish tests Ibn al-Jarmi: questioning on knowledge, then inspecting physical appearance (mustache, nails, pubic hair). This shows Jish's attempt to find fault.
Ibn al-Jarmi passes all tests, leading to his release.
4
Reflect on the divine punishment
Consider Jish's later illness (leprosy and intestinal distress) and his dream of arrows. The people interpret it as the effect of Ibn al-Jarmi's supplication.
This illustrates the theme of divine justice through a righteous du'a.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم أبو بكر الدمشقى الزاهد المعروف بابن الجرمي رحمه الله ) أخبرنا الشيخ أبو محمد هبة الله بن أحمد المعدل قال ثنا أبو محمد عبد العزيز بن أحمد الصوفى قال حدثني نجا بن أحمد العطار قال توفي أبو بكر محمد بن الجرمي بن الحسين المقرى في صفر سنة ست وثلاثين وأربعماية حدث عن ابن أبي الزمزام والفضل بن جعفر وغيرهما قال عبد العزيز وكان يذهب إلى مذهب أبي الحسن الأشعري رحمه الله سمعت الشيخ الفقيه الإمام أبا الحسن علي بن المسلم السلمي رحمه الله يحكي عن بعض شيوخه أن أبا بكر بن الجرمي كان من الآمرين بالمعروف الناهين عن المنكر وأنه صادف في بعض الأيام أحمالا من الخمر قد أتى بها لوالى دمشق جيش بن الصمصامة الوالي من قبل المصريين وكان جيش هذا عاتياً جباراً فأراقها كلها أبو بكر عند بيت لهيا وكان جيش ينزل في بيت لهيا فأبلغ جيش الخبر فأمر بإحضاره فسأله عن أشياء من القرآن والحديث والفقه فوجده عالماً بما سأله عنه فنظر إلى شاربه فوجده مقصوصاً ثم نظر إلى أظافيره فوجدها مقلمة فأمر بأن ينظر إلى عانته فوجده قد حلق عانته فقال له جيش اذهب فقد نجوت مني ولو وجدت فيك ما أحتج به عليك لم تنج هذا معنى ما ذكره وسمعته أيضا يقول لما بلغ جيش في مرضه الذي ابتلي به ما بلغ وكان أصابه الجذام وألقى ما في من أمعائه حتى كان يقول لأصحابه أقتلوني وأريحوني من الحياة لشدة ما كان يناله من الألم قال لأصحابه رأيت كأن أهل دمشق كلهم رموني بالسهام فأخطوني غير رجل واحد أصابني سهمه ولا أسميه لأني لو سميته لعبدوه أهل دمشق فكانوا يرون أن الذي أصابه دعوة ابن الجرمي هذا وكان جيش سفاكاً للدم شديد التعدي على الأموال مظهر السب للسلف
And among them is Abu Bakr al-Dimashqi the ascetic, known as Ibn al-Jarmi, may Allah have mercy on him. The shaykh Abu Muhammad Hibatallah ibn Ahmad al-Muaddil informed us, saying: Abu Muhammad Abd al-Aziz ibn Ahmad al-Sufi narrated to us, saying: Naja ibn Ahmad al-Attar narrated to me, saying: Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Jarmi ibn al-Husayn al-Muqri died in Safar of the year 436 AH. He narrated from Ibn Abi al-Zamzam, al-Fadl ibn Ja'far, and others. Abd al-Aziz said: He followed the school of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, may Allah have mercy on him. I heard the shaykh al-faqih al-imam Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn al-Muslim al-Sulami, may Allah have mercy on him, relate from some of his shaykhs that Abu Bakr ibn al-Jarmi was among those who enjoin right and forbid wrong. And that one day he encountered loads of wine that had been brought to the governor of Damascus, Jish ibn al-Samsama, the governor from the Egyptians. This Jish was a tyrannical tyrant. Abu Bakr spilled all of it at Bayt Lahya, and Jish used to stay in Bayt Lahya. The news reached Jish, so he ordered him brought. He questioned him about matters from the Quran, hadith, and fiqh, and found him knowledgeable about what he asked. Then he looked at his mustache and found it clipped, then looked at his nails and found them trimmed, and he ordered that his pubic region be examined, and found that he had shaved it. So Jish said to him: 'Go, for you have been saved from me. If I had found in you something with which I could argue against you, you would not have been saved.' This is the gist of what he mentioned. And I also heard him say: When Jish's illness – with which he was afflicted – reached its peak, he had contracted leprosy and expelled what was in his intestines, so much so that he would say to his companions: 'Kill me and relieve me from life' due to the severity of the pain that afflicted him. He said to his companions: 'I saw as if all the people of Damascus shot arrows at me, and they missed me except one man whose arrow hit me. I will not name him, for if I named him, the people of Damascus would worship him.' And they used to think that what had struck him was the supplication of Ibn al-Jarmi. And Jish was a shedder of blood, extremely oppressive over wealth, openly insulting the Salaf.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry that first provides basic information about Abu Bakr ibn al-Jarmi: his death date, his teachers, and his adherence to the Ash'ari school. The core of the entry is a dramatic story. It narrates that Ibn al-Jarmi, as an enforcer of good and forbidder of evil, courageously poured out loads of wine that were intended for the governor Jish. When brought before Jish, the governor tested him by questioning him on religious sciences and then examining his physical appearance: his mustache was clipped, his nails trimmed, and his pubic region shaved – all signs of Islamic practice according to the Sunnah. Finding no fault, the governor released him, stating that if he had found any evidence against him, he would not have escaped punishment. The passage then describes Jish's later suffering: he contracted leprosy and severe diarrhea, to the point of begging for death. In his agony, he had a dream or vision that all the people of Damascus shot arrows at him; all missed except one man, whose arrow struck him. He refused to name that man, fearing that people would worship him. The people believed that the one who struck him was the supplication (curse) of Ibn al-Jarmi. The entry ends by emphasizing Jish's tyranny, oppression, and open insult of the Salaf. This story illustrates the power of a righteous scholar's supplication and God's justice in answering it against a tyrant.

Key Terms
ابن الجرمي
جيش بن الصمصامة
بيت لهيا
سفاك للدم
الدعوة المستجابة
الآمرون بالمعروف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That only the ruler's power matters in a society, and scholars have no real authority.
Correction: The story shows that a humble scholar's supplication can bring down a tyrant, demonstrating that spiritual authority can overcome political power.

Checkpoint
What did Abu Bakr ibn al-Jarmi do that angered the governor Jish?
A) He rebuked the governor publicly
B) He poured out loads of wine brought for the governor
C) He refused to pray for the governor
D) He insulted the governor's ancestors

Key Takeaway
The story of Ibn al-Jarmi and Jish illustrates that a righteous scholar's supplication is a powerful weapon against tyranny, capable of bringing divine justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn al-Jarmi risked his life to spill wine for the sake of Allah. Is there a situation in your life where you are tolerating a sin (in yourself or in your community) because you fear the consequences of speaking out? What would it take for you to act like Ibn al-Jarmi, trusting in Allah's protection? Commit to one specific action this week.
?
Jish's suffering was so severe that he asked his companions to kill him. Yet even then, he refused to name the one whose supplication he believed caused it, fearing people would worship him. When you face a trial that you suspect is a punishment for your sins, do you stubbornly avoid the lesson, or do you humble yourself and repent? Are you more concerned with your reputation or with returning to Allah?
?
The people of Damascus understood that a single du'a from a righteous man brought down a tyrant. Do you truly believe that your prayers have such power, or have you reduced du'a to a mere ritual? Think of a specific oppression or injustice you see around you. Will you make a sincere, heartfelt du'a against it, fully trusting that Allah can respond, and then wait for His decree with patience?

The Righteous Scholar: Abu Bakr al-Dimashqi
Biographical entry with a vivid narrative of enjoining good.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the life and example of Abu Bakr al-Dimashqi, including his Ash'ari affiliation, his practice of enjoining good and forbidding evil, and the story of his encounter with the tyrannical governor Jaysh.

Core Idea
Righteousness and knowledge are a shield against oppression.

This lesson introduces a remarkable scholar from the Ash'ari tradition, Abu Bakr al-Dimashqi. His biography illustrates how a scholar's firm adherence to truth and Islamic etiquette can disarm even the most tyrannical ruler. The story also highlights the power of sincere supplication.

Guided Path
1
Read the biography carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the key details: death date, teachers, and especially the story with the governor.
2
Analyze the story's structure
Notice how Ibn al-Jarmi's actions (spilling wine) led to his arrest. The governor tested his knowledge and observed his grooming. Identify why the governor let him go.
The governor looked for a legal pretext but found none.
3
Reflect on the lesson
Consider the ending: the governor's illness and his own admission that Ibn al-Jarmi's supplication caused it. What does this teach about divine justice and the power of da'wa?
This reinforces that Allah supports the righteous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And among them is Abu Bakr al-Dimashqi al-Zahid, known as Ibn al-Jarmi, may Allah have mercy on him. The Shaykh Abu Muhammad Hibat Allah ibn Ahmad al-Mu'addil informed us, saying: Abu Muhammad 'Abd al-'Aziz ibn Ahmad al-Sufi narrated to us, saying: Naja ibn Ahmad al-'Attar narrated to me, saying: Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Jarmi ibn al-Husayn al-Muqri' died in Safar of the year six and thirty and four hundred (436 AH). I heard the Shaykh, the faqih, the Imam Abu al-Hasan 'Ali ibn al-Muslim al-Sulami, may Allah have mercy on him, narrating from some of his shaykhs that Abu Bakr ibn al-Jarmi was among those who enjoin good and forbid evil, and that one day he encountered loads of wine that had been brought for the governor of Damascus, Jaysh ibn al-Samsama, the governor from the Egyptians, and this Jaysh was a tyrannical despot. So Jaysh said to him: Go, you have escaped from me. If I had found in you anything with which I could argue against you, you would not have escaped. This is the meaning of what he mentioned. And I also heard him say: When Jaysh in his illness with which he was afflicted reached what he reached, and he was struck with leprosy and expelled what was in his intestines until he would say to his companions: Kill me and give me rest from life, due to the severity of the pain that afflicted him. He said to his companions: I saw that all the people of Damascus shot arrows at me, and they missed me, except for one man whose arrow hit me, and I will not name him, because if I named him, the people of Damascus would worship him. And they came to believe that what had hit him was the supplication (da'wa) of Ibn al-Jarmi. And Jaysh was a shedder of blood, extremely oppressive in seizing wealth, and openly reviling the Salaf.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry on Abu Bakr al-Dimashqi al-Zahid, known as Ibn al-Jarmi. It begins with his death date (Safar 436 AH) and his teachers: Ibn Abi al-Zimzam and al-Fadl ibn Ja'far. A key detail is that he followed the Ash'ari school (madhhab). Then follows a vivid story illustrating his practice of enjoining good and forbidding evil. He encountered wine destined for the tyrannical governor Jaysh bin al-Samsama and poured it out. When summoned, Jaysh tested him on religious knowledge and found him learned. Then the governor examined Ibn al-Jarmi's personal grooming: trimmed mustache, clipped nails, and shaved pubic area—signs of compliance with Islamic fitra and cleanliness. Unable to find any legal pretext to punish him, the governor let him go, saying he had 'escaped.' The passage then reports that the governor later suffered a terrible illness (leprosy and intestinal issues), and he himself attributed his suffering to the supplication of Ibn al-Jarmi. The narrative underscores the virtue of the scholar and the downfall of the oppressor, consistent with aqidah themes of divine justice and the power of righteous supplication.

Key Terms

أمر بالمعروف ونهي عن المنكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Ibn al-Jarmi directly cursed or prayed for the governor's illness.
Correction: The story does not imply that Ibn al-Jarmi cursed the governor; rather it suggests his righteous act and supplication were effective. The governor himself connected his suffering to Ibn al-Jarmi's da'wa.

Key Takeaway
A scholar's knowledge and adherence to divine commands can be a means of protection, and the supplication of the righteous holds weight with Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When the governor tested Ibn al-Jarmi, he found him faultless in both knowledge and practice. If your life were subjected to the same scrutiny by someone who wishes you harm, would they find any area where you are neglecting Islamic obligations—perhaps in your speech, your daily habits, or your treatment of others? What specific neglected practice will you restore starting today?
?
Ibn al-Jarmi risked his life by pouring out wine destined for a tyrant. Reflect on a 'wine' in your own life—a situation where you know something is wrong but you remain silent because of fear of consequences. Will you speak up or act this week, knowing that righteousness can be a shield?
?
The governor, on his deathbed, confessed that one righteous man's supplication struck him. If you were in the governor's place, whose supplication would be hitting you because of the wrongs you have committed against them? Make a list of people you may have wronged and resolve to seek their forgiveness today, before it is too late.

The Scholar: Imam Abu Muhammad al-Juwayni
Biographical details on scholarly identity and works

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly identity, major works, and educational influence of Imam Abu Muhammad al-Juwayni as described in the biographical source.

Core Idea
Imam Abu Muhammad al-Juwayni was a towering scholar whose works shaped Islamic jurisprudence and tafsir.

This passage introduces us to a major figure in Islamic intellectual history: Imam Abu Muhammad al-Juwayni, father of the more famous Imam al-Haramayn. The text provides his full lineage, an impressive list of titles, and details about his students and writings. As you read, pay attention to the breadth of his expertise—he was not only a jurist but also a grammarian, exegete, and usuli—and the impact he had on those who studied under him.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on his identity and titles
Read the Arabic text and translation focusing on his full name, lineage, and the numerous titles given to him (Imam, Pillar of Islam, faqih, usuli, adib, nahwi, mufassir, unique of his time).
These titles reflect his mastery across multiple Islamic sciences.
2
Identify his students and teaching atmosphere
Notice that many leading imams graduated under him, and his classroom was marked by seriousness and respect due to his piety and dignity.
The phrase 'لا يجري بين يديه إلا الجد والحث والتحريض على التحصيل' highlights his teaching environment.
3
List his major works
The text mentions four works: al-Tabsira, al-Tadhkira, Mukhtasar al-Mukhtasar (in fiqh), and the Great Tafsir which covers ten types per verse. Count them and understand their significance.
The tafsir is described as 'الكبير' and each verse is treated from ten angles.
4
Note his death and legacy
He died in Dhu al-Qa'da 438 AH, and the text states he left no equal in comprehensive excellence.
This sets the stage for the high praise in the next lesson.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم الإمام أبو محمد الجويني والد الإمام أبي المعالي رحمه الله ) كتب إلى الشيخ أبو الحسن عبد الغافر بن إسماعيل الفارسي قال : ثنا عبد الله بن يوسف بن عبد الله بن يوسف بن محمد بن جبويه الجويني ثم النيسابوري أبو محمد الإمام ركن الإسلام ، الفقيه الأصولي الأديب النحوى المفسر ، أوحد زمانه تخرج به جماعة من أئمة الإسلام ، وكان لصيانته وديانته مهيباً محترماً بين التلامذة فلا يجري بين يديه إلا الجد والحث والتحريض على التحصيل له في الفقه تصانيف كثيرة الفوائد : مثل التبصرة والتذكرة ، ومختصر المختصر ، وله التفسير الكبير المشتمل على عشرة أنواع في كل آية ، توفى في ذي القعدة سنة ثمان وثلاثين وأربعمائة ولم يخلف مثله في استجماعه . وسمعت خالي الإمام أبا سعيد يعني عبد الواحد بن عبد الكريم القشيري يقول كان أئمتنا في عصره والمحققون من أصحابنا يعتقدون فيه من الكمال والفضل والخصال الحميدة أنه لو جاز أن يبعث الله نبياً في عصره لما كان إلا هو من حسن طريقته وورعه وزهده وديانته في كمال فضله . وحدثني القاضي أبو بكر يحيى بن إبراهيم بن أحمد ابن محمد السلماسي بدمشق عن أبيه أبي طاهر قال قال أبو علي الحسن بن نصر ابن كاكا المرندي الفقيه حدثني أبو القسم بن منصور بن رامس على ذكر أبي محمد الجويني ، قال من ألطف أخلاقه وأحسنها أنه رجل ركين الجملة وافر العقل جاد في أمره كله ، لا ترى فيه شيئاً من الرعونة لمساواة ظاهره باطنه وموافقة سره علانيته وزهده في الرياسة التي صارت تطلبه وهو يهرب منها وترغب فيه وهو يبعد عنها
And among them is the Imam Abu Muhammad al-Juwayni, the father of the Imam Abu al-Ma'ali, may Allah have mercy on him. He wrote to the Shaykh Abu al-Hasan 'Abd al-Ghafir ibn Isma'il al-Farisi, saying: 'Abd Allah ibn Yusuf ibn 'Abd Allah ibn Yusuf ibn Muhammad ibn Jibwayh al-Juwayni, then al-Nisaburi, Abu Muhammad, the Imam, the Pillar of Islam, the jurist (faqih), the usuli, the man of letters (adib), the grammarian (nahwi), the exegete (mufassir), the unique one of his time. A group of imams of Islam graduated under him. Due to his integrity and piety, he was awe-inspiring and respected among students; nothing but seriousness, encouragement, and urging to study would take place in his presence. He has many beneficial works in fiqh, such as al-Tabsira, al-Tadhkira, and Mukhtasar al-Mukhtasar. He also has the Great Tafsir which includes ten types in each verse. He died in Dhu al-Qa'da of the year eight and thirty and four hundred (438 AH). He left no one like him in comprehensive excellence. And I heard my maternal uncle, the Imam Abu Sa'id, meaning 'Abd al-Wahid ibn 'Abd al-Karim al-Qushayri, say: Our imams in his time and the verified scholars among our companions believed that he possessed such perfection, virtue, and praiseworthy qualities that if it were possible for Allah to send a prophet in his time, there would be none other than him, due to the excellence of his way, his piety, his asceticism, and his religiousness in the perfection of his virtue. And the judge Abu Bakr Yahya ibn Ibrahim ibn Ahmad ibn Muhammad al-Salmasi narrated to me in Damascus from his father Abu Tahir, who said: Abu 'Ali al-Hasan ibn Nasr ibn Kaka al-Marandi al-Faqih said: Abu al-Qasim ibn Mansur ibn Ramis narrated to me concerning the memory of Abu Muhammad al-Juwayni, saying: Among the subtlest and best of his character traits is that he was a man of dignified bearing, complete intellect, earnest in all his affairs. You would see no trace of foolishness in him, due to the equality of his outward and inward, and the harmony of his secret and public, and his asceticism regarding leadership which began to seek him while he fled from it, and it desired him while he kept away from it.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry on Imam Abu Muhammad al-Juwayni, the father of the renowned Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni. It begins with his full name and lineage: 'Abd Allah ibn Yusuf... al-Juwayni then al-Nisaburi. He is given many titles: Imam, Pillar of Islam, jurist, usuli, adib, nahwi, mufassir, and unique of his time. The text notes that many leading imams graduated under him, and that his piety and dignity commanded respect among students, so that only seriousness and encouragement for learning prevailed in his presence. It then lists his major works: al-Tabsira, al-Tadhkira, Mukhtasar al-Mukhtasar in fiqh, and a Great Tafsir that treats each verse from ten different angles. His death is recorded in Dhu al-Qa'da 438 AH, with the remark that he left no equal in comprehensive excellence. The next part is a citation from his nephew (or son-in-law) Imam Abu Sa'id al-Qushayri, who reports that the leading scholars of the age considered that if it were possible for Allah to send a prophet in that era, it would be al-Juwayni, due to his perfect method, piety, asceticism, and religiosity. This is an extraordinary praise, but it is phrased as a hypothetical (law jāza), affirming his human perfection while maintaining the impossibility of prophethood after the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him). Finally, an anecdote from Abu 'Ali al-Hasan ibn Nasr describes his character: dignified, intelligent, earnest, with perfect harmony between his inner and outer states, and a strong aversion to leadership—which he fled from even as it pursued him.

Key Terms

الفقيه الأصولي
التفسير الكبير
التبصرة
التذكرة

Key Takeaway
Imam Abu Muhammad al-Juwayni was a polymath whose scholarly output and students made him a pivotal figure in Islamic learning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says that due to his piety, students were serious and respectful in his presence. Is your own learning environment marked by such reverence? What habits will you change to create that atmosphere?
?
He was called 'the unique of his time' (أوحد زمانه). If you were to be remembered for one distinctive contribution to Islam, what would it be? What step will you take this week to move toward that goal?
?
He produced a tafsir that covered each verse from ten angles. How deep is your own engagement with the Quran? Are you satisfied with superficial understanding, or will you commit to studying a tafsir like his?

Character and Virtue of Imam al-Juwayni
Testimonies on character and virtue

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the extraordinary character and virtue of Imam Abu Muhammad al-Juwayni as praised by his peers, including the famous hypothetical prophecy statement.

Core Idea
Imam al-Juwayni's character was so perfect that scholars said if a prophet could be sent, he would be the one.

This lesson delves into the remarkable character of Imam Abu Muhammad al-Juwayni. The source contains a stunning praise from his nephew, Imam Abu Sa'id al-Qushayri, who reports that the leading scholars believed that if it were hypothetically possible to send a new prophet, al-Juwayni would be the one—a statement that underscores his complete virtue while respecting the finality of prophethood. An additional anecdote from another scholar describes his dignified bearing, harmony between inner and outer self, and his avoidance of leadership that pursued him. As you read, consider the high level of piety and asceticism that earned him such acclaim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on his character
Read the Arabic and translation from 'وسمعت خالي' to the end. Focus on the two main testimonies: one from Imam Abu Sa'id al-Qushayri and one from Abu 'Ali al-Hasan ibn Nasr.
The passage combines a high praise from relatives and a character sketch from a contemporary.
2
Analyze the hypothetical prophecy praise
Understand the wording 'لو جاز أن يبعث الله نبياً في عصره لما كان إلا هو'. This is a rhetorical exaggeration to show his perfection, not a literal belief in continued prophethood.
The phrase 'لو جاز' indicates it is a hypothetical impossibility.
3
Identify his character traits from the anecdote
From the second testimony, list the traits: dignified bearing (رجل ركين الجملة), complete intellect (وافر العقل), earnestness (جاد في أمره كله), harmony of outward and inward, and asceticism regarding leadership.
Note the contrast: he flees from leadership while it seeks him.
4
Reflect on the virtue of asceticism
Consider how his asceticism (زهده) in worldly leadership is a key theme. The text emphasizes that he runs away from leadership while it runs after him.
This shows his humility and focus on the akhira.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the praise 'if a prophet could be sent, it would be him' means Imam al-Juwayni claimed prophethood or that prophethood continues.

A) He was the best scholar of his time.

D) He was the most knowledgeable in tafsir.

?

?

?
The scholars used extreme hyperbole to describe al-Juwayni's character. If people were to use a similar rhetorical exaggeration about your character, what would they struggle to find to praise? What change must you make today to move toward that kind of reputation?

Opening a Biographical Entry: The Isnad in Tarajim
Opening of a biographical entry with an isnad chain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical Arabic biographical works begin entries with a chain of transmission (isnad) and how to identify the key figures in that chain.

Core Idea
The isnad is the backbone of Islamic biographical literature.

This lesson presents the opening of a biographical entry on Abu al-Qasim al-Hamdani al-Baghdadi from Ibn 'Asakir's Tabyin Kidhb al-Muftari. Even though the biography is cut off, its beginning reveals the method of citing authorities through a chain of narrators—a hallmark of Islamic scholarship. We will examine the isnad and identify the narrators involved.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to understand the basic meaning.
Notice the word 'كه' which is unclear; the meaning may be 'who said' or similar.
2
Identify the subject and the two shaykhs
The biography begins by naming the person (Abu al-Qasim al-Hamdani) and then two shaykhs who transmitted the account. Note their full names and titles.
The two shaykhs are the first generation in the chain.
3
Trace the chain down to al-Khatib al-Baghdadi
Follow the chain from the two shaykhs through 'Ali and Muhammad to Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn 'Ali ibn Thabit al-Khatib, the famous historian al-Khatib al-Baghdadi.
Al-Khatib al-Baghdadi is a key figure; the chain continues further to 'Ali ibn al-Hasan.
4
Reflect on the role of isnad in biographies
Ask yourself why Ibn 'Asakir includes a chain of transmission for a biography. This is a method of verifying the source of information, just like in hadith.
This connects to the learning objective.
5
Consider the incomplete nature
The biography cuts off after the isnad. This is likely due to a page break or excerpt. The chain itself is valid as far as it goes.
Incomplete does not mean unreliable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم أبو القسم بن أبي عثمان الهمدانی البغدادی رحمه الله كه أخبرنا الشيخان أبو الحسن علي بن أحمد بن قبيس وأبو منصور حمد بن عبد الملك بن خيرون ، قال على ثنا وقال محمد أنا أبو بكر احمد بن على بن ثابت الخطيب قال : على بن الحسن بن محمد المنتاب
And among them is Abu al-Qasim ibn Abi 'Uthman al-Hamdani al-Baghdadi, may Allah have mercy on him, who [said]? the two shaykhs Abu al-Hasan 'Ali ibn Ahmad ibn Qubays and Abu Mansur Hamd ibn 'Abd al-Malik ibn Khayrun informed us, 'Ali said: narrated to us, and Muhammad said: Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn 'Ali ibn Thabit al-Khatib narrated to us, saying: 'Ali ibn al-Hasan ibn Muhammad al-Muntab
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a biographical entry on Abu al-Qasim al-Hamdani al-Baghdadi. It is incomplete in the current window. The text provides his name and then begins an isnad (chain of transmission) involving two shaykhs: Abu al-Hasan 'Ali ibn Ahmad ibn Qubays and Abu Mansur Hamd ibn 'Abd al-Malik ibn Khayrun. The chain continues through Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn 'Ali ibn Thabit al-Khatib (the famous historian al-Khatib al-Baghdadi) to 'Ali ibn al-Hasan ibn Muhammad al-Muntab. The biography cuts off there, so we do not have the full content about this scholar's life, works, or death.

Key Terms
أبو القسم
أبو الحسن علي بن أحمد بن قبيس
أبو منصور حمد بن عبد الملك بن خيرون
أبو بكر أحمد بن علي بن ثابت الخطيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The biography is worthless because it is incomplete.
Correction: The incomplete isnad is due to a page break; the chain itself is valid as far as it goes, and the excerpt still demonstrates the method of transmission.

Checkpoint
Which famous historian appears in the citation chain for Abu al-Qasim al-Hamdani?
A) Ibn Asakir
B) al-Khatib al-Baghdadi
C) al-Dhahabi
D) Ibn al-Jawzi

Key Takeaway
A biographical entry in classical works begins with an isnad, linking the author to earlier authorities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the chain of narrators in this passage, do you feel a personal connection to these scholars? How does knowing that your knowledge is transmitted through such chains affect your responsibility to preserve and transmit it accurately?
?
The isnad shows that every piece of knowledge has a source. Think of one belief you hold about Islam. Can you trace it back to an authentic source as clearly as this chain? If not, what step will you take today to verify it?
?
The passage cuts off, leaving the biography incomplete. In your own life, are there areas where your understanding of your faith is incomplete because you haven't traced it back to its original sources? Commit to seeking one missing link this week.

The Life and Virtues of Abu Muhammad al-Juwayni
Ibn Asakir details the life, learning, and virtues of Abu Muhammad al-Juwayni.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be familiar with the life, scholarly works, and character of Abu Muhammad al-Juwayni as recorded by Ibn Asakir, and understand the significance of the hyperbolic praise in classical biographical literature.

Core Idea
Abu Muhammad al-Juwayni was a scholar so virtuous that contemporaries said he could have been a prophet.

Ibn Asakir presents the biography of Abu Muhammad al-Juwayni, a towering Ash‘ari scholar. This passage not only lists his titles and works but also reveals his character through the eyes of his peers. As you read, notice how the text combines factual record with hyperbolic praise, a common feature of medieval Islamic biographical writing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to get an overview of the biographical details.
The Arabic has minor OCR errors, but the meaning is clear.
2
Identify key biographical facts
List the scholar's full name, titles, areas of expertise, and major works mentioned. Note his death date.
Titles include 'Pillar of Islam' and 'unique of his time'.
3
Examine the two testimonies
First testimony by al-Qushayri: what extreme praise is given? Second testimony by al-Marandi: what character traits are highlighted?
Compare the hyperbolic language with the specific moral qualities.
4
Understand the rhetorical hyperbole
The phrase 'if it were permissible for Allah to send a prophet' is not a literal belief but a conventional expression of utmost piety.
This guards against misunderstanding the text.
5
Reflect on the purpose of the biography
Ask: why is this biography included in a work defending Ash‘ari theology? How does it establish al-Juwayni's authority?
The biography bolsters the credibility of the scholars cited in the book.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الله ابن يوسف بن عبد الله بن يوسف بن محمد بن جبويه الجويني ثم عبد النيسابوري أبو محمد الإمام ركن الإسلام ، الفقيه الأصولي الأديب النحوى المفسر ، أو حد زمانه تخرج به جماعة من أئمة الإسلام ، وكان لصيانته وديانته مهيباً محترماً بين التلامذة فلا يحرى بين يديه إلا الجد والحث والتحريض علي التحصيل له في الفقه تصانيف كثيرة الفوائد : مثل التبصرة والتذكرة ، ومختصر المختصر ، وله التفسير الكبير المشتمل على عشرة أنواع فى كل آية ، توفى في ذى القعدة سنة ثمان وثلاثين وأو بعمائة ولم يخلف مثله في استجماعه . وسمعت خالى الإمام أبا سعيد يعني عبد الواحد بن عبد الكريم القشيري يقول كان أئمتنا في عصره والمحققون من أصحابنا يعتقدون فيه من الكمال والفضل والخصال الحميدة أنه لو جاز أن يبعث الله نبياً في عصره لما كان إلا هو من حسن طريقته وورعه وزهده وديانته في كمال فضله . وحدثني القاضي أبو بكر يحيى بن إبراهيم بن أحمد ابن محمد السلماسي بدمشق عن أبيه أبى طاهر قال قال أبو على الحسن بن نصر ابن كاكا المرندى الفقيه حدثني أبو القسم بن منصور بن رامس على ذكر أبي محمد الجويني ، قال من ألطف أخلاقه وأحسنها أنه رجل ركين الجملة وافر العقل جاد في أمره كله ، لا ترى فيه شيئاً من الرعونة لمساواة ظاهره باطنه وموافقة سره علانيته وزهده في الرياسة التى صارت تطلبه وهو يهرب منها وترغب فيه وهو يبعد عنها
Allah ibn Yusuf ibn Abdullah ibn Yusuf ibn Muhammad ibn Jubawayh al-Juwayni then Abd al-Naysaburi, Abu Muhammad, the Imam, the Pillar of Islam, the jurist, the usuli, the literary scholar, the grammarian, the exegete, the unique one of his time. A group of Imams of Islam graduated under him. Due to his integrity and piety, he was majestic and respected among students; nothing but seriousness, encouragement, and urging towards learning occurred in his presence. He authored many beneficial works in fiqh, such as al-Tabsira, al-Tadhkira, and Mukhtasar al-Mukhtasar. He also wrote the Great Exegesis which includes ten types in each verse. He died in Dhu al-Qa‘dah of the year 438 AH, and no one like him in comprehensiveness was left behind. And I heard my maternal uncle, Imam Abu Sa‘id, meaning ‘Abd al-Wahid ibn ‘Abd al-Karim al-Qushayri, say: 'Our Imams of his time and the verifiers among our companions believed in him such perfection, virtue, and praiseworthy qualities that if it were permissible for Allah to send a prophet in his time, it would be none but him, due to the excellence of his way, his piety, his asceticism, and his religiosity in the perfection of his virtue.' And Qadi Abu Bakr Yahya ibn Ibrahim ibn Ahmad ibn Muhammad al-Salamasi informed me in Damascus from his father Abu Tahir, who said: Abu ‘Ali al-Hasan ibn Nasr ibn Kaka al-Marandi al-Faqih said: Abu al-Qasim ibn Mansur ibn Ramas narrated to me, mentioning Abu Muhammad al-Juwayni, saying: 'Among the most subtle and excellent of his character traits is that he was a man of solid composition, abundant intellect, earnest in all his affairs. You see no trace of pomposity in him, because his outward matched his inward, his secret agreed with his public, and he was ascetic regarding leadership, which came seeking him while he fled from it and desired him while he distanced himself from it.'
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry (tarjama) for Abu Muhammad al-Juwayni, presented as part of a collection of biographies of prominent Ash‘ari scholars. The passage begins by stating his name, titles, and scholarly specialization: he was an Imam, the Pillar of Islam, a jurist, usuli, literary scholar, grammarian, and exegete, and the unique figure of his time. It then notes his role as a teacher from whom many Imams graduated. His conduct in the classroom was characterized by gravity and seriousness, with no frivolity. His scholarly output included major works in fiqh and a voluminous exegesis. The date of his death is given as 438 AH, and he left no equal in comprehensiveness. The entry then quotes two testimonies: first, his nephew Imam Abu Sa‘id al-Qushayri reported that the leading scholars of the age believed al-Juwayni possessed such perfect virtue that if divine dispensation allowed a prophet after Muhammad, he would be that man. This is a hyperbolic but standard expression of reverence in biographical literature, emphasizing his exceptional piety, asceticism, and religiosity. Second, a judge reports a description of al-Juwayni’s character: he was solid, intelligent, earnest, and free from pomposity because his interior and exterior were aligned, and he fled from leadership even as it sought him. The biography thus establishes al-Juwayni’s scholarly authority, moral integrity, and spiritual excellence, reinforcing his credibility as an Ash‘ari authority.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'لو جاز أن يبعث الله نبياً' is a literal claim that prophethood could resume after Muhammad.
Correction: It is a rhetorical exaggeration expressing extreme piety, not a literal belief in the possibility of a new prophet.

Checkpoint

B) That if it were permissible for Allah to send a prophet in his time, it would be none but him
C) That he was a wali (saint) with miraculous powers
D) That he would be the Mahdi at the end of time

Key Takeaway
The biography of Abu Muhammad al-Juwayni presents him as an ideal scholar combining immense knowledge, piety, and humility, serving as a model for Ash‘ari scholars and reinforcing the credibility of the tradition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Juwayni's outward matched his inward, and his secret agreed with his public. When was the last time you acted one way in private and another in public? What would need to change for your inward and outward to be truly consistent?
?
He fled from leadership while it sought him. Is there a position, status, or recognition you are chasing that might actually be a test for your sincerity? Are you ready to flee from it if it distracts you from Allah?
?
The scholars of his time considered him of such perfection that they said if it were permissible, he could have been a prophet. Do you seek to be seen as perfect, or do you seek Allah's pleasure even if it means being overlooked and forgotten?

Biography of Abu al-Qasim ibn Abi Uthman al-Hamdani
Ibn Asakir records the life, teachers, and death of Abu al-Qasim ibn Abi Uthman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the biographical details of Abu al-Qasim ibn Abi Uthman al-Hamdani as recorded by Ibn Asakir, including his chain of transmission, teachers, and personal attributes.

Core Idea
A scholar's biography in the Tabyin establishes trustworthiness and defends against detractors.

Ibn Asakir carefully documents the life of Abu al-Qasim ibn Abi Uthman to affirm his reliability as a transmitter. This biography, with its chain of reporting, list of teachers, and description of character, serves as a model for how traditional scholarship authenticates its narrators. Let's examine how this entry builds confidence in the subject's credentials.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the structure emerge before interpreting.
Notice the chain of narration at the beginning.
2
Identify the sections
Break the passage into: (1) chain of narration, (2) identification of the subject, (3) list of teachers, (4) personal description and dates.
Each section serves a purpose in authentication.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why Ibn Asakir includes his own writing from the subject and the description 'hasan al-madhhab'. How do these details defend the scholar?
This connects to the broader purpose of the Tabyin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم أبو القسم بن أبي عثمان الهمدانی البغدادی رحمه الله که أخبرنا الشيخان أبو الحسن علي بن أحمد بن قبيس وأبو منصور حمد بن عبد الملك بن خيرون ، قال على ثنا وقال محمد أنا أبو بكر احمد بن على بن ثابت الخطيب قال : على بن الحسن بن محمد المنتاب . أبو القسم المعروف بابن أبي عثمان الدقاق ، سمع أبا بكر بن مالك القطيعي ، وأبا محمد ابن ماسی وعلی بن محمد بن سعيد الرزاز ، وأبا الحسين الزينبي ، وعبد العزيز ابن جعفر الخرقي وأبا حفص بن الزيات وعلي ابن إبراهيم بن أبي عزة العطار ، وأبا الحسين بن البواب وأبا بكر بن شاذان ، . كتبت عنه وكان شيخاً صالحا صدوقاً دينا حسن المذهب يسكن نهر القلايين ، وسألته عن مولده فقال فى ذى الحجة من سنة خمس وخمسين وثلاثماية ومات في يوم السبت السابع والعشرين من شهر ربيع الأول سنة أربعين وأربعماية ودفن في مقبرة الشونيزي
And among them is Abu al-Qasim ibn Abi Uthman al-Hamdani al-Baghdadi, may Allah have mercy on him. He narrated to us: the two shaykhs Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Ahmad ibn Qubays and Abu Mansur Hamd ibn Abd al-Malik ibn Khayrun — Ali said: he narrated to us, and Muhammad said: Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Ali ibn Thabit al-Khatib narrated to us, he said: Ali ibn al-Hasan ibn Muhammad al-Muntab [?]. Abu al-Qasim known as Ibn Abi Uthman al-Daqqaq heard from: Abu Bakr ibn Malik al-Qati‘i, and Abu Muhammad ibn Masi, and Ali ibn Muhammad ibn Sa‘id al-Razzaz, and Abu al-Husayn al-Zaynabi, and Abd al-Aziz ibn Ja‘far al-Kharqi, and Abu Hafs ibn al-Zayyat, and Ali ibn Ibrahim ibn Abi ‘Izza al-‘Attar, and Abu al-Husayn ibn al-Bawwab, and Abu Bakr ibn Shadhan. I wrote from him, and he was a righteous shaykh, trustworthy, religious, of good madhhab, residing in Nahr al-Qalayin. I asked him about his birth, and he said: in Dhu al-Hijjah of the year 355 AH. He died on Saturday, the 27th of Rabi‘ al-Awwal, 440 AH, and was buried in the Shawinizi cemetery.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry for Abu al-Qasim ibn Abi Uthman al-Hamdani al-Baghdadi. The entry begins by listing the chain of reporters through whom the author (Ibn Asakir) received this information, involving two shaykhs and al-Khatib al-Baghdadi. Then the main figure is identified by his name and nickname (Ibn Abi Uthman al-Daqqaq). A list of his teachers is provided, including several well-known hadith transmitters. The author states that he personally wrote from Ibn Abi Uthman, and describes him as righteous, trustworthy, religious, and of good madhhab (likely meaning Sunni and specifically Ash‘ari). His place of residence is given as Nahr al-Qalayin. Finally, his birth year (355 AH) and death date (440 AH) are recorded, along with his burial place in the Shawinizi cemetery. The purpose of such entries in this work is to establish the reliability and credentials of the scholars being defended, as well as to document the chains of transmission for their teachings.

Correction: In this context, 'حسن المذهب' generally indicates sound Sunni orthodoxy, not a particular madhhab.

The Life and Creed of Abu Ja'far al-Samnani
Ibn Asakir recounts the life and scholarly credentials of Abu Ja'far al-Samnani, Qadi of Mosul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the biographical details of Abu Ja'far al-Samnani, including his scholarly credentials, his adherence to the Ash'ari creed, and his role as Qadi of Mosul.

Core Idea
A Hanafi jurist who embodied Ash'ari orthodoxy and scholarly generosity.

This biography from Ibn 'Asakir presents a model of Sunni scholarship: a trusted narrator, a generous teacher, and a judge who held Ash'ari beliefs while following the Hanafi school in jurisprudence. Through this entry, we see how early Sunni scholars blended adherence to a madhhab with a sound creed rooted in the Ash'ari tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for the biographical style of Ibn 'Asakir. Note the isnad and the descriptive terms.
The opening 'ر' is part of 'ومنهم' as a continuation from the previous biography.
2
Identify the subject and his teachers
Find the full name of al-Samnani and the scholars he narrated from, including al-Daraqutni. This establishes his authority.
3
Understand his virtues and creed
The text describes him as trustworthy, knowledgeable, virtuous, generous, and eloquent. Note that he was Hanafi in fiqh and Ash'ari in usul (creed).
'Hasan al-kalam' means eloquence, not 'ilm al-kalam.
4
Learn about his study circle and death
He held a scholarly council in his home attended by jurists. He died in Mosul as qadi on a specific date. The details confirm his legacy.
The date is Monday, 6 Rabi' al-Awwal 444 AH.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ر ومنهم أبو جعفر السمنانى قاضى الموصل رحمه الله ) أخبرنا الشريف أبو القسم على بن إبراهيم الحسيني ، والشيخ أبو الحسن على ابن أحمد الغساني ، وأبو منصور محمد بن عبد الملك الخيروني قالوا قال لنا أبو بكر أحمد بن علی بن ثابت الخطيب : محمد بن أحمد بن محمد بن أحمد بن محمد بن محمود أبو جعفر القاضي السمنانى سكن بغداد وحدث بها عن على ابن عمر السكرى وأبي الحسن الدارقطنى وأبى القسم بن جبابة وغيرهم من البغداديين ، وعن نصر بن بن الخليل الموصلى ، كتبت عنه وكان ثقة عالماً فاضلاً سخيا حسن الكلام عراقي المذهب حنيفيا ويعتقد في الأصول مذهب الأشعرى وكان له فى داره مجلس نظر فحضره الفقهاء . وأحمد ويتكلمون سمعت السمنانى سئل عن مولده فقال ولدت في سنة إحدى وستين وثلاثماية ومات بالموصل وهو علي القضاء بها وكانت وفاته في يوم الاثنين السادس من شهر ربيع الأول من سنة أربع وأربعين وأربعماية .
And among them is Abu Ja‘far al-Samnani, Qadi of Mosul, may Allah have mercy on him. Al-Sharif Abu al-Qasim Ali ibn Ibrahim al-Husayni, and Shaykh Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Ahmad al-Ghassani, and Abu Mansur Muhammad ibn Abd al-Malik al-Khayruni informed us, they said: Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Ali ibn Thabit al-Khatib said to us: Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn Mahmud, Abu Ja‘far al-Qadi al-Samnani resided in Baghdad and narrated there from Ali ibn Umar al-Sukkari, Abu al-Hasan al-Daraqutni, Abu al-Qasim ibn Jabbaba, and others from the Baghdadis, and from Nasr ibn al-Khalil al-Mawsili. I wrote from him, and he was trustworthy, knowledgeable, virtuous, generous, eloquent in speech, of Iraqi madhhab, a Hanafi, and believed in the principles of the Ash‘ari school. He had a study circle in his home which jurists attended. And Ahmad [?] and they would speak. I heard al-Samnani asked about his birth, and he said: I was born in the year 361 AH. He died in Mosul while holding the qadiship there, and his death occurred on Monday, the 6th of Rabi‘ al-Awwal, 444 AH.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry for Abu Ja‘far al-Samnani, the Qadi of Mosul. The entry begins with the chain of transmission from three shaykhs back to al-Khatib al-Baghdadi, who provides the biography. The full name of al-Samnani is given, and it is noted that he resided in Baghdad and narrated hadith from several prominent scholars, including al-Daraqutni. The author states that he personally wrote from him and describes him with multiple virtues: trustworthy, knowledgeable, virtuous, generous, eloquent, Hanafi in fiqh, and Ash‘ari in creed (usul). He held a scholarly council (majlis nazar) in his home attended by jurists. His birth year is given as 361 AH, and his death as 444 AH in Mosul while serving as qadi. The biography serves to establish his scholarly authority and his adherence to Ash‘ari theology, which is a key purpose of this work—to defend the orthodoxy of Ash‘ari scholars.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think 'حسن الكلام' refers to skill in 'ilm al-kalam (theological argumentation).
Correction: In this context, 'حسن الكلام' means eloquence in speech, not expertise in kalam theology. The text distinguishes between his fiqh and his creed, so 'الكلام' here is general speech.

Key Takeaway
Abu Ja'far al-Samnani was a trustworthy Hanafi jurist and Ash'ari theologian who served as Qadi of Mosul, exemplifying the integration of fiqh and creed in Sunni orthodoxy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Samnani was described as 'generous' (sakhiyyan). Is there a specific person or cause in your life where you are withholding generosity? What would change if you gave openly, as he did?
?
He combined Hanafi fiqh with Ash'ari creed without contradiction. In what area of your life do you compartmentalize your faith? What would it take for you to integrate your beliefs and actions more fully?
?
His study circle was attended by jurists, implying he sought knowledge throughout his life. When did you last intentionally place yourself in a learning environment that challenged your existing views? What is one step you can take this week to do so again?

Biography of Ibn Abi Uthman al-Daqqaq
Ibn Asakir records the biography of Abu al-Qasim Ibn Abi Uthman al-Daqqaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the typical structure of a biographical entry in classical Islamic history, using the example of Ibn Abi Uthman al-Daqqaq.

Core Idea
A scholar's biography preserves their teachers, integrity, and place in history.

This lesson presents a short biographical entry from Ibn Asakir's work. It shows how scholars documented the lives of hadith transmitters, noting their teachers, moral qualities, and dates. The passage is a window into the scholarly network of 4th-5th century Baghdad.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once, noting the names and phrases. Then read the English translation to ensure comprehension.
Pay attention to the phrase 'حسن المذهب'.
2
Identify key biographical elements
List the elements: the scholar's name, his teachers, his character description, his residence, his birth and death dates, and his burial place.
This is the standard format for such entries.
3
Reflect on the term 'حسن المذهب'
Consider why Ibn Asakir includes this phrase and what it implies about the scholar's doctrinal stance.
It often indicates adherence to Ash'ari theology.
4

These details establish reliability and connection.

أبو القسم المعروف بابن أبي عثمان الدقاق ، سمع أبا بكر بن مالك القطيعي ، وأبا محمد ابن ماسی وعلی بن محمد بن سعيد الرزاز ، وأبا الحسين الزينبي ، وعبد العزيز ابن جعفر الخرقي وأبا حفص بن الزيات وعلي ابن إبراهيم بن أبي عزة العطار ، وأبا الحسين بن البواب وأبا بكر بن شاذان ، . كتبت عنه وكان شيخاً صالحا صدوقاً دينا حسن المذهب يسكن نهر القلايين ، وسألته عن مولده فقال فى ذى الحجة من سنة خمس وخمسين وثلاثماية ومات في يوم السبت السابع والعشرين من شهر ربيع الأول سنة أربعين وأربعماية ودفن في مقبرة الشونيزي
Abu al-Qasim known as Ibn Abi Uthman al-Daqqaq heard from Abu Bakr ibn Malik al-Quti'i, Abu Muhammad Ibn Masi, Ali ibn Muhammad ibn Sa'id al-Razzaz, Abu al-Husayn al-Zaynabi, Abd al-Aziz ibn Ja'far al-Kharqi, Abu Hafs ibn al-Zayyat, Ali ibn Ibrahim ibn Abi Azza al-Attar, Abu al-Husayn ibn al-Bawwab, and Abu Bakr ibn Shadhan. I wrote from him and he was a righteous elder, truthful, religious, of good madhhab (doctrine), residing in Nahr al-Qallayin. I asked him about his birth and he said in Dhu al-Hijjah of the year 355 AH, and he died on Saturday the 27th of Rabi' al-Awwal of the year 440 AH, and was buried in the Shunizi cemetery.

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry from Ibn Asakir's work. It introduces Abu al-Qasim al-Daqqaq, listing his teachers (shuyukh) from whom he heard hadith. Ibn Asakir confirms his reliability with the description 'righteous, truthful, religious, of good madhhab' — the last phrase likely indicating his adherence to Ash'ari doctrine. His residence in Nahr al-Qallayin and his birth and death dates are given, placing him in the late 4th and early 5th centuries AH. The entry shows the chain of transmission and the scholarly network.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Think about the scholars you have learned from. Do you know the names and backgrounds of those who taught you? What steps are you taking to verify the authenticity of the knowledge you receive?
?
If a historian were to write a biography of you today, what would they say about your character? Would they describe you as 'truthful, religious, and of sound doctrine'? What specific habits could you change to align with that description?
?
The scholars of the past carefully preserved their teachers' names and dates for the sake of Allah's religion. How seriously do you take the accuracy of the Islamic knowledge you convey to others? Are you willing to trace back every piece of information to its source? What will you do starting today to improve in this area?

The Biography of Abu Ja'far al-Samnani: A Hanafi-Ash'ari Scholar
Ibn Asakir records the biography of Abu Ja'far al-Samnani, qadi of Mosul, noting his teachers, character, and dual affiliation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a scholar could combine Hanafi fiqh with Ash'ari theology, as exemplified by Abu Ja'far al-Samnani.

Core Idea
A scholar can be Hanafi in fiqh and Ash'ari in creed — no contradiction.

Ibn Asakir records the life of Abu Ja'far al-Samnani, a qadi of Mosul who was both a Hanafi jurist and an Ash'ari theologian. This biography illustrates the compatibility of these schools in early Islamic scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Let the author's own words come through.
The passage is a standard biographical entry from Ibn Asakir's work.
2
Identify key descriptors
Note the descriptions of al-Samnani: his teachers (especially al-Daraqutni), his personal qualities (trustworthy, knowledgeable, generous), and his affiliations (Hanafi fiqh, Ash'ari usul).
The phrase 'عراقي المذهب حنيفيا' indicates his fiqh madhhab, and 'يعتقد في الأصول مذهب الأشعرى' indicates his theology.
3
Reflect on compatibility
Consider that the passage presents a Hanafi-Ash'ari combination as unproblematic. This challenges the idea that these schools are incompatible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم أبو جعفر السمنانى قاضى الموصل رحمه الله ) أخبرنا الشريف أبو القسم على بن إبراهيم الحسيني ، والشيخ أبو الحسن على ابن أحمد الغساني ، وأبو منصور محمد بن عبد الملك الخيروني قالوا قال لنا أبو بكر أحمد بن علی بن ثابت الخطيب : محمد بن أحمد بن محمد بن أحمد بن محمد بن محمود أبو جعفر القاضي السمنانى سكن بغداد وحدث بها عن على ابن عمر السكرى وأبي الحسن الدارقطنى وأبى القسم بن جبابة وغيرهم من البغداديين ، وعن نصر بن بن الخليل الموصلى ، كتبت عنه وكان ثقة عالماً فاضلاً سخيا حسن الكلام عراقي المذهب حنيفيا ويعتقد في الأصول مذهب الأشعرى وكان له فى داره مجلس نظر فحضره الفقهاء . ويتكلمون سمعت السمنانى سئل عن مولده فقال ولدت في سنة إحدى وستين وثلاثماية ومات بالموصل وهو علي القضاء بها وكانت وفاته في يوم الاثنين السادس من شهر ربيع الأول من سنة أربع وأربعين وأربعماية
And among them is Abu Ja'far al-Samnani, qadi of Mosul, may God have mercy on him. Al-Sharif Abu al-Qasim Ali ibn Ibrahim al-Husayni, and Shaykh Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Ahmad al-Ghassani, and Abu Mansur Muhammad ibn Abd al-Malik al-Khayruni said: Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Ali ibn Thabit al-Khatib told us: Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn Mahmud Abu Ja'far al-Qadi al-Samnani resided in Baghdad and narrated there from Ali ibn Umar al-Sukari, Abu al-Hasan al-Daraqutni, Abu al-Qasim ibn Jubaba, and others from Baghdad, and from Nasr ibn al-Khalil al-Mawsili. I wrote from him and he was trustworthy, knowledgeable, virtuous, generous, good in speech, Iraqi in madhhab, Hanafi, and in matters of principles (usul) he believed the madhhab of al-Ash'ari. He had a study circle in his house which jurists attended and spoke. I heard al-Samnani asked about his birth and he said: I was born in the year 361 AH, and he died in Mosul while serving as its qadi, and his death was on Monday the 6th of Rabi' al-Awwal in the year 444 AH.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents the biography of Abu Ja'far al-Samnani. Ibn Asakir transmits through a chain to al-Khatib al-Baghdadi, who describes al-Samnani's residence in Baghdad, his teachers including the famous hadith scholar al-Daraqutni, and his own characteristics: trustworthy, knowledgeable, generous, good in speech. His legal affiliation is noted as Hanafi ('Iraqi madhhab') and his theological school as Ash'ari ('in usul he believed the madhhab of al-Ash'ari'). He held a study circle (majlis nazar) attended by jurists. His birth year 361 and death year 444 are given, showing he lived into the 5th century.

Correction: This biography shows a Hanafi jurist who explicitly held Ash'ari theology, proving compatibility.
